# test_data.txt - Genetics Chatbot Test Data
# Format: Question | Category | Detailed Answer

What is the difference between DNA and RNA? | basics | DNA is double-stranded with deoxyribose sugar and thymine base, stable for long-term storage. RNA is single-stranded with ribose sugar and uracil base, versatile for various functions including protein synthesis.
How does translation differ from transcription? | processes | Transcription copies DNA to RNA in nucleus, producing mRNA. Translation decodes mRNA to protein at ribosomes in cytoplasm, using tRNA to bring amino acids. Transcription is DNA-dependent RNA synthesis; translation is RNA-dependent protein synthesis.
Explain dominant vs recessive alleles | inheritance | Dominant alleles express their trait when one copy is present (heterozygous). Recessive alleles require two copies (homozygous) for expression. In heterozygous individuals, dominant masks recessive phenotype, but recessive allele can still be transmitted.
What causes Down syndrome? | disorders | Down syndrome (trisomy 21) is caused by an extra copy of chromosome 21, usually due to nondisjunction during meiosis. Features include intellectual disability, characteristic facial features, and increased risk of heart defects and other health issues.
How does natural selection lead to adaptation? | evolution | Natural selection leads to adaptation as individuals with heritable traits better suited to their environment survive and reproduce more successfully. Over generations, advantageous traits increase in frequency, making populations better adapted.
What is the role of tRNA in protein synthesis? | structures | tRNA (transfer RNA) carries specific amino acids to ribosomes during translation. Each tRNA has an anticodon complementary to mRNA codon and corresponding amino acid, ensuring correct amino acid sequence in polypeptide chain.
Explain how CRISPR-Cas9 edits genes | technology | CRISPR-Cas9 uses guide RNA to target specific DNA sequences. Cas9 enzyme cuts both DNA strands at target site. Cells repair the break, potentially introducing mutations (non-homologous end joining) or precise edits (homology-directed repair with donor DNA).
What is polygenic inheritance? | inheritance | Polygenic inheritance involves multiple genes contributing to a single trait, resulting in continuous variation (like height, skin color). Traits show normal distribution in population, with additive effects of many genes plus environmental influences.
How do viruses replicate genetically? | processes | Viruses replicate by hijacking host cell machinery. After attachment and entry, viral genome is replicated using viral or host enzymes, viral proteins are synthesized, and new virions assemble and exit to infect new cells.
What is a silent mutation? | mutations | A silent mutation changes DNA sequence but doesn't alter amino acid sequence due to codon degeneracy. Usually occurs at third base position. While protein unchanged, it can affect mRNA stability, splicing, or translation efficiency.
Explain X-chromosome inactivation | epigenetics | X-chromosome inactivation equalizes gene dosage between XX females and XY males. In each female cell, one X chromosome is randomly condensed into Barr body, silencing most genes. Process involves Xist RNA coating and epigenetic modifications.
How does gel electrophoresis separate DNA? | technology | Gel electrophoresis separates DNA fragments by size using electric field. DNA (negatively charged) migrates toward positive electrode through agarose gel matrix. Smaller fragments move faster, creating distinct bands visualized with DNA-binding dyes.
What is a missense mutation? | mutations | A missense mutation changes nucleotide, substituting one amino acid for another in protein. Effects range from benign to severe depending on location and chemical property change (conservative vs non-conservative substitution).
Explain founder effect with example | evolution | Founder effect occurs when small group establishes new population. Example: Amish community with high Ellis-van Creveld syndrome due to few founders carrying recessive allele. Reduced genetic variation and increased recessive disorders characterize such populations.
How do restriction enzymes recognize DNA? | technology | Restriction enzymes recognize specific palindromic DNA sequences (4-8 base pairs). Type II enzymes cut within recognition site, producing sticky ends (overhangs) or blunt ends. Bacterial defense system modified for biotechnology.
What is a frameshift mutation? | mutations | Frameshift mutation inserts or deletes nucleotides not in multiples of three, shifting reading frame. Alters all downstream codons, usually producing nonfunctional protein with premature stop codon. Often more severe than point mutations.
Explain how stem cells differentiate | cell_biology | Stem cells differentiate through signaling pathways that activate specific transcription factors, changing gene expression patterns. External signals (morphogens) and internal programs determine cell fate, leading to specialized cell types.
What is genetic anticipation? | inheritance | Genetic anticipation describes earlier onset or increased severity of disorder in successive generations. Seen in triplet repeat disorders like Huntington's disease, where unstable repeats expand during meiosis, especially paternal transmission.
How does DNA packaging occur? | structures | DNA wraps around histone proteins forming nucleosomes, which coil into 30nm fibers, loop into domains attached to nuclear matrix, and further condense into chromosomes during cell division. Packaging regulates gene accessibility.
What is a transgenic crop? | engineering | Transgenic crops have foreign genes inserted for desired traits. Examples: Bt corn produces bacterial toxin against insects; Roundup Ready soybeans tolerate glyphosate herbicide; Golden Rice produces beta-carotene (vitamin A precursor).
Explain how PCR amplifies DNA | technology | PCR amplifies DNA exponentially through cycles: denaturation (95°C separates strands), annealing (50-65°C primers bind), extension (72°C Taq polymerase synthesizes new strands). Each cycle doubles DNA amount, yielding millions of copies.
What is a nonsense mutation? | mutations | A nonsense mutation changes codon to stop codon (UAA, UAG, UGA), causing premature translation termination. Usually produces truncated, nonfunctional protein degraded by cellular quality control systems.
How do homeotic genes control development? | genetics | Homeotic genes (like Hox genes) encode transcription factors controlling body plan organization. They specify segment identity along anterior-posterior axis; mutations cause homeotic transformations (body parts developing in wrong locations).
What is the Hardy-Weinberg principle used for? | evolution | Hardy-Weinberg principle provides null model for population genetics. Deviations from expected genotype frequencies indicate evolutionary forces at work: selection, mutation, drift, migration, or non-random mating. Used to calculate allele frequencies.
Explain DNA microarray technology | technology | DNA microarrays measure gene expression by hybridizing fluorescently labeled cDNA to DNA probes on chip. Signal intensity indicates expression level. Used for profiling gene expression patterns in different conditions or tissues.
What is a conditional knockout? | research | Conditional knockout inactivates gene in specific tissues or developmental stages using Cre-lox or similar systems. Cre recombinase excises DNA between loxP sites, allowing spatial/temporal control of gene function studies.
How does alternative splicing increase diversity? | expression | Alternative splicing combines exons differently from same pre-mRNA, producing multiple protein isoforms with distinct functions. Human genes average 3-4 splice variants, greatly expanding proteomic complexity from ~20,000 genes.
What is allopatric speciation? | evolution | Allopatric speciation occurs when populations become geographically isolated, preventing gene flow. Genetic divergence through mutation, selection, and drift leads to reproductive isolation even if populations later come into contact.
Explain how DNA proofreading works | processes | DNA polymerase has 3'→5' exonuclease activity that removes mismatched nucleotides during replication. After adding nucleotide, polymerase checks fit; if incorrect, excises and replaces it, reducing error rate to ~1 in 10^7 bases.
What is RNA splicing? | processes | RNA splicing removes introns and joins exons from pre-mRNA. Catalyzed by spliceosome (snRNPs and proteins) recognizing splice sites. Alternative splicing allows different exon combinations from single gene.
How do microRNAs regulate genes? | expression | MicroRNAs (miRNAs) bind complementary sequences in target mRNA 3'UTRs, leading to translational repression or mRNA degradation. Fine-tune gene expression post-transcriptionally, important in development, differentiation, and disease.
What is a quantitative trait locus? | genetics | QTL is chromosomal region associated with variation in quantitative trait. Identified through linkage analysis or GWAS. Contains one or multiple genes contributing to trait, plus possible regulatory elements.
Explain convergent evolution in mammals | evolution | Marine mammals (dolphins, whales) and fish evolved streamlined bodies, fins, and tail flukes independently for aquatic life. Marsupial and placental mammals show similar adaptations (wolves vs thylacines) in different continents.
What is a genetic mosaic? | genetics | Genetic mosaic has cells with different genotypes from single zygote, due to mutation during development. Examples: females with X-inactivation patterns; segmental neurofibromatosis; cancer with somatic mutations in tumor cells.
How does epigenetics influence cancer? | epigenetics | Cancer involves epigenetic alterations: global DNA hypomethylation (genomic instability), promoter hypermethylation (tumor suppressor silencing), histone modification changes. Epigenetic therapies target these changes (DNA methyltransferase inhibitors).
What is a molecular clock? | evolution | Molecular clock estimates evolutionary time based on mutation rate assumption. Neutral mutations accumulate at roughly constant rate; sequence differences between species proportional to time since divergence, calibrated with fossil records.
Explain how yeasts are used in genetics | research | Yeasts (S. cerevisiae) are model eukaryotes for genetics: rapid reproduction, haploid/diploid cycles, easy genetic manipulation, conserved cellular processes. Used to study cell cycle, gene regulation, and human disease genes.
What is a genetic association study? | research | Genetic association studies compare allele frequencies between cases and controls to identify variants associated with disease or trait. Genome-wide association studies (GWAS) scan millions of SNPs across genome.
How does bacterial conjugation work? | processes | Conjugation transfers DNA between bacteria via pilus. F+ donor transfers F plasmid to F- recipient. Hfr strains integrate F factor into chromosome, transferring chromosomal genes. Mechanism of horizontal gene transfer.
What is a syntenic block? | research | Syntenic block is conserved chromosomal region between species with similar gene order and content. Identified through comparative genomics, indicating evolutionary conservation and functional importance.
Explain how mutations cause cancer | disorders | Cancer mutations activate oncogenes (RAS, MYC) or inactivate tumor suppressors (TP53, RB). Accumulate over lifetime from environmental mutagens, replication errors, or inherited predisposition. Driver mutations confer growth advantage.
What is gene conversion? | genetics | Gene conversion transfers genetic information between homologous sequences without reciprocal exchange. During meiosis, heteroduplex DNA repair favors one allele, changing genotype ratio from expected Mendelian segregation.
How does mitochondrial inheritance differ? | inheritance | Mitochondrial DNA is maternally inherited (only from mother), doesn't recombine, has higher mutation rate, and shows heteroplasmy (mixed populations within cell). Disorders show maternal transmission pattern and variable expression.
What is a knockout organism? | research | Knockout organism has specific gene made nonfunctional to study its role. Created by homologous recombination inserting selectable marker into target gene. Reveals gene functions in development, physiology, and disease.
Explain how DNA vaccines work | technology | DNA vaccines inject plasmid DNA encoding antigen into muscle. Host cells take up DNA, express antigen, stimulating both antibody and cellular immune responses. Advantages: stability, no refrigeration, easy production.
What is a haplogroup? | genetics | Haplogroup is group of similar haplotypes sharing common ancestor with single nucleotide polymorphism. Used in human genetics to trace maternal (mtDNA) or paternal (Y-chromosome) lineages and migration patterns.
How does RNA editing create diversity? | expression | RNA editing alters nucleotide sequence after transcription. A-to-I editing changes adenosine to inosine (read as guanosine). Creates protein variants, regulates splicing, and modifies coding potential from same genomic sequence.
What is a genetic screen in model organisms? | research | Genetic screen identifies genes affecting phenotype by examining mutant collections. Forward screen: mutagenize, select mutants, map gene. Reverse screen: target specific gene, observe phenotype. Used in flies, worms, yeast.
Explain how epigenetics is inherited | epigenetics | Some epigenetic marks survive meiosis and are transmitted to offspring. Examples: DNA methylation patterns at imprinted loci; histone modifications; paramutation in plants. Provides mechanism for transgenerational inheritance.
What is a copy number variant? | genetics | CNV is DNA segment present in variable copy number compared to reference genome. Includes deletions, duplications, multiplications. Can affect gene dosage, contribute to disease susceptibility, and drive evolutionary change.
How do zinc finger nucleases work? | technology | Zinc finger nucleases fuse zinc finger DNA-binding domains to FokI nuclease. Custom zinc fingers recognize specific sequences; FokI dimerizes and cuts DNA, enabling targeted gene editing before CRISPR development.
What is a balanced polymorphism? | evolution | Balanced polymorphism maintains multiple alleles in population through balancing selection. Mechanisms: heterozygote advantage (sickle cell trait against malaria), frequency-dependent selection, variable environment across space/time.
Explain how metagenomics studies microbiomes | research | Metagenomics sequences all DNA from environmental sample (gut, soil, ocean). Identifies microbial composition, functional potential, and interactions without culturing. Reveals vast microbial diversity and ecosystem functions.
What is a genetic circuit? | engineering | Genetic circuits engineer gene networks to perform logical operations in cells. Components: promoters, repressors, activators. Applications: biosensors, metabolic engineering, synthetic biology for production of compounds.
How does RNA polymerase II initiate transcription? | processes | RNA Pol II initiates transcription with help of general transcription factors forming pre-initiation complex at promoter. TBP binds TATA box, TFIIH phosphorylates Pol II CTD, allowing promoter escape and elongation.
What is a telomere and its function? | structures | Telomeres are repetitive sequences (TTAGGG in humans) at chromosome ends, protecting against degradation and fusion. Shorten with each division; telomerase extends them in stem cells, germ cells, and cancer cells.
Explain how genetic algorithms solve problems | applications | Genetic algorithms evolve solutions through selection, crossover, mutation. Initialize population, evaluate fitness, select parents, produce offspring with genetic operators, iterate. Used for optimization, scheduling, design.
What is a chimera in genetics? | research | Genetic chimera contains cells from different zygotes. Natural: blood group chimerism from twin exchange in utero. Experimental: mixing embryos to study development; human-animal chimeras for organ generation research.
How does DNA methylation silence genes? | epigenetics | DNA methylation at promoter CpG islands recruits methyl-binding proteins that attract histone deacetylases and chromatin remodelers, forming condensed heterochromatin inaccessible to transcription machinery, silencing gene expression.
What is a genetic correlation study? | research | Genetic correlation estimates shared genetic basis between traits using twin studies, family data, or GWAS. Reveals pleiotropy where genes influence multiple traits, informing biological pathways and comorbidity.
Explain how optogenetics controls neurons | technology | Optogenetics inserts light-sensitive ion channels (channelrhodopsin for activation, halorhodopsin for inhibition) into neurons. Light pulses of specific wavelengths control neural activity with millisecond precision.
What is a morphogen gradient? | genetics | Morphogen gradient forms when secreted signaling molecules diffuse from source, creating concentration gradient. Cells respond differently based on threshold concentrations, patterning tissues during embryonic development.
How does single-cell RNA-seq work? | technology | Single-cell RNA-seq isolates individual cells, converts mRNA to barcoded cDNA, sequences, and maps reads to quantify gene expression per cell. Reveals cellular heterogeneity, developmental trajectories, rare cell types.
What is a genetic network motif? | research | Network motif is recurrent circuit pattern in genetic networks. Examples: feed-forward loops (coherent/incoherent), single-input modules, dense overlapping regulons. Perform specific regulatory functions like filtering noise.
Explain how kin selection favors altruism | evolution | Kin selection favors altruism when cost to actor < benefit to recipient × relatedness (Hamilton's rule). Genes for altruism spread if they help relatives who likely carry same alleles, increasing inclusive fitness.
What is a genetic assimilation experiment? | research | Genetic assimilation: Waddington exposed fly pupae to heat shock, selecting for crossveinless phenotype over generations. Eventually phenotype appeared without heat shock, showing genetic fixation of induced trait.
How does FISH identify chromosomes? | technology | FISH uses fluorescent probes complementary to specific DNA sequences. After hybridization, fluorescence microscopy visualizes probe location on chromosomes. Applications: detecting aneuploidy, translocations, gene amplifications.
What is a genetic load calculation? | evolution | Genetic load = 1 - (mean fitness of population / fitness of optimal genotype). Calculated from allele frequencies and selection coefficients. Measures population's genetic burden from deleterious mutations.
Explain how plasmids are used in cloning | technology | Plasmids are cloning vectors: restriction sites insert foreign DNA; origin of replication for propagation; selectable marker (antibiotic resistance) for identifying transformants; promoter for expression in host cells.
What is a synthetic lethal interaction? | research | Synthetic lethality: two non-lethal mutations combined cause death. Used to identify drug targets: inhibit gene synthetic lethal with cancer mutation, selectively killing tumor cells (PARP inhibitors in BRCA-mutant cancers).
How does chromatin immunoprecipitation work? | technology | ChIP isolates DNA bound by specific proteins. Crosslink protein-DNA, fragment DNA, immunoprecipitate with antibody to protein of interest, reverse crosslinks, sequence DNA. Identifies transcription factor binding sites.
What is a genome-wide association study? | research | GWAS scans millions of SNPs across genomes of many individuals to find statistical associations with traits/diseases. Identifies risk loci, but most associated variants are in non-coding regions with regulatory effects.
Explain how DNA barcoding identifies species | applications | DNA barcoding amplifies and sequences standardized gene region (COI for animals). Compares sequence to reference database for species identification. Applications: biodiversity monitoring, forensics, detecting invasive species.
What is a genetic switch in development? | expression | Developmental genetic switches commit cells to specific fates. Example: sex determination switch: SRY gene on Y chromosome activates male pathway; absence leads to female development. Often involve binary decisions.
How does Muller's ratchet affect asexual populations? | evolution | Muller's ratchet: asexual populations accumulate deleterious mutations irreversibly. Without recombination to separate good and bad mutations, each generation has at least as many mutations as best individual from previous generation.
What is a ribozyme therapeutic? | applications | Ribozyme therapeutics use catalytic RNA to target specific mRNA for cleavage. Examples: angiozyme targeted VEGF receptor in cancer trials; hepatitis delta virus ribozyme studied for antiviral therapy. Challenges: delivery, stability.
Explain how imprinting causes Prader-Willi syndrome | disorders | Prader-Willi syndrome results from loss of paternally expressed genes on chromosome 15q11-13. Causes: paternal deletion (70%), maternal uniparental disomy (25%), imprinting defects. Features: hypotonia, hyperphagia, obesity, intellectual disability.
What is a haplotype block? | genetics | Haplotype block is chromosomal region with limited historical recombination, so SNPs within are highly correlated (in linkage disequilibrium). Important for GWAS: tag SNPs represent whole block, reducing needed markers.
How does DNA sequencing detect mutations? | technology | DNA sequencing determines nucleotide order, identifying mutations by comparing to reference. Methods: Sanger (chain termination), next-generation (massively parallel), third-generation (long reads). Detects SNPs, indels, structural variants.
What is a genetic algorithm in machine learning? | applications | Genetic algorithms in ML optimize parameters or architectures. Encode solutions as chromosomes, evaluate fitness (accuracy), select best, apply crossover and mutation to create next generation, converge to optimal solution.
Explain how homeobox genes pattern the body | genetics | Homeobox genes (Hox clusters) are expressed in overlapping patterns along anterior-posterior axis. Their combination gives each segment unique identity, controlling development of appropriate structures in correct locations.
What is a protoplast fusion experiment? | research | Protoplast fusion combines genomes of different plant species by removing cell walls and fusing membranes with PEG or electric pulses. Creates somatic hybrids for combining desirable traits bypassing sexual incompatibility.
How does epigenetic inheritance challenge neo-Darwinism? | epigenetics | Epigenetic inheritance shows acquired traits can be transmitted, challenging strict separation of germline and soma. Provides additional inheritance mechanism and faster adaptation than DNA sequence changes alone.
What is a genetic correlation between disorders? | research | Genetic correlation between disorders indicates shared genetic risk factors. Example: schizophrenia and bipolar disorder share genetic variants. Informs comorbidity, suggests common biological pathways for treatment targets.
Explain how RNA interference silences genes | technology | RNAi introduces double-stranded RNA homologous to target gene. Dicer processes to siRNAs, RISC complex incorporates guide strand, cleaves complementary mRNA. Used for gene knockdown in research and therapy development.
What is a morphogen in limb development? | genetics | Morphogens pattern limb development: Sonic hedgehog (SHH) from zone of polarizing activity specifies digit identity (posterior); FGFs from apical ectodermal ridge promote outgrowth; Wnt7a specifies dorsal-ventral axis.
How does CRISPR diagnostics detect viruses? | technology | CRISPR diagnostics (like SHERLOCK) use Cas13 collateral RNA cleavage activity. Upon target recognition, Cas13 cleaves reporter RNA, producing detectable signal. Can detect attomolar concentrations of viral RNA.
What is a genetic network in disease? | research | Disease genetic networks connect genes through protein interactions, pathways, or co-expression. Hub genes are potential therapeutic targets. Network medicine approaches identify modules dysregulated in disease.
Explain how allopolyploidy creates new species | evolution | Allopolyploidy combines genomes of different species. Hybrid sterile due to chromosome pairing issues, but chromosome doubling restores fertility, creating instant new species reproductively isolated from both parents.
What is a conditional allele in mouse models? | research | Conditional allele has loxP sites flanking critical exon. Cre recombinase expression in specific tissues or times excises flanked region, knocking out gene only where and when Cre is expressed.
How does DNA repair prevent cancer? | processes | DNA repair pathways correct damage from mutagens. Defects increase mutation rate, leading to oncogene activation and tumor suppressor inactivation. Examples: BRCA in homologous repair, XP genes in nucleotide excision repair.
What is a genetic switch in biotechnology? | engineering | Synthetic genetic switches control gene expression in response to inputs. Examples: toggle switch (bistable on/off), repressilator (oscillatory), logic gates. Used in metabolic engineering, biosensors, therapeutic delivery.
Explain how molecular clocks date evolutionary events | evolution | Molecular clock assumes neutral mutations accumulate at constant rate. Calculate divergence time = (genetic distance) / (2 × mutation rate × generation time). Calibrated with fossil dates or known geological events.
What is a chimera for organ generation? | research | Human-animal chimeras created by injecting human stem cells into animal embryos, aiming to grow human organs in animals for transplantation. Ethical and technical challenges: contribution to brain, immune rejection.
How does chromatin remodeling regulate genes? | epigenetics | Chromatin remodeling complexes (SWI/SNF, ISWI) slide, eject, or restructure nucleosomes using ATP. Make DNA accessible (euchromatin) or inaccessible (heterochromatin) to transcription factors, dynamically regulating gene expression.
What is a synteny analysis in comparative genomics? | research | Synteny analysis aligns genomes to identify conserved gene order blocks. Reveals evolutionary rearrangements (inversions, translocations), gene duplications, and functionally constrained regions across species.
Explain how RNA polymerase II processes mRNA | processes | RNA Pol II coordinates mRNA processing: 5' capping co-transcriptionally, splicing facilitated by CTD phosphorylation recruiting splicing factors, 3' cleavage and polyadenylation. Links transcription to RNA processing.
What is a restriction fragment length polymorphism? | genetics | RFLP detects DNA sequence variations affecting restriction sites. Digest DNA with enzyme, separate fragments by size; pattern differences indicate polymorphisms. Early method for genetic mapping and fingerprinting.
How does genetic compensation explain knockout phenotypes? | genetics | Genetic compensation: knockout of gene upregulates related genes or alternative pathways, masking expected phenotype. Complicates interpretation of knockout studies; may require double/triple knockouts.
What is a ribosome profiling experiment? | research | Ribosome profiling (Ribo-seq) sequences ribosome-protected mRNA fragments, mapping translating ribosomes genome-wide. Measures translation efficiency, identifies novel open reading frames, detects regulated translation.
Explain how DNA fingerprinting solves crimes | applications | DNA fingerprinting compares STR profiles from crime scene to suspect. Multiple STR loci analyzed, extremely low probability of identical profiles in unrelated individuals. Used for identification, paternity, missing persons.
What is a transposon mutagenesis screen? | research | Transposon mutagenesis randomly inserts transposons into genome, disrupting genes. Sequence insertion sites in mutants to identify genes essential for phenotype. High-throughput functional genomics in bacteria, flies, mice.
How does the lac operon demonstrate gene regulation? | expression | Lac operon shows inducible regulation: repressor binds operator, blocking transcription; lactose (inducer) binds repressor, inactivating it; cAMP-CAP complex activates transcription when glucose low. Classic model of prokaryotic regulation.
What is a genetic switch in evolution? | evolution | Evolutionary genetic switches involve changes in regulatory elements altering gene expression patterns. Example: stickleback fish EDA gene regulatory changes reduce armor plates in freshwater vs marine populations.
Explain how Muller's ratchet is overcome | evolution | Sexual reproduction overcomes Muller's ratchet through recombination, separating deleterious mutations from beneficial ones. Allows selection to eliminate bad mutations without losing good alleles in same individuals.
What is a ribozyme in the ribosome? | structures | Ribosomal peptidyl transferase center is a ribozyme—catalytic RNA that forms peptide bonds during protein synthesis. Evidence for RNA world: key cellular function performed by RNA without protein catalyst.
How does imprinting affect growth disorders? | disorders | Imprinted genes often regulate growth. Loss of maternal expression (Igf2 receptor) or paternal expression (Igf2) disrupts normal growth. Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome (overgrowth) and Silver-Russell syndrome (growth retardation) involve imprinting defects.
What is a haplotype in disease association? | genetics | Disease-associated haplotypes contain multiple correlated SNPs; one may be causal variant. Identifying haplotype blocks simplifies GWAS. Example: HLA haplotypes associated with autoimmune diseases like type 1 diabetes.
How does nanopore sequencing work? | technology | Nanopore sequencing passes DNA through protein pore; each base disrupts ionic current uniquely. Reads long fragments (100kb+), real-time analysis, portable devices. Used for field sequencing, structural variant detection.
What is a genetic algorithm for drug design? | applications | Genetic algorithms optimize drug candidates: encode molecules as strings, evaluate binding affinity (fitness), mutate and combine structures, evolve toward better binders. De novo design of novel therapeutic compounds.
Explain how homeotic genes are conserved | genetics | Homeotic (Hox) genes conserved across animals with similar roles in anterior-posterior patterning. Gene order on chromosome correlates with expression pattern along body axis (colinearity). Fundamental to animal development.
What is a protoplast for genetic transformation? | research | Protoplasts take up foreign DNA easily for genetic transformation. Methods: PEG-mediated, electroporation, microinjection. Regenerate whole plants from transformed protoplasts, creating transgenic plants.
How does transgenerational epigenetic inheritance work? | epigenetics | Epigenetic marks (DNA methylation, histone modifications) escape erasure during germline reprogramming, transmitted to offspring. Examples: Dutch Hunger Winter effects on grandchildren's health; paramutation in plants.
What is a genetic correlation with environment? | research | Gene-environment correlation: genes influence exposure to environments (active, evocative, passive). Example: genetic predisposition to reading leads to more reading practice. Complicates nature-nurture studies.
Explain how RNA therapeutics work | applications | RNA therapeutics: antisense oligonucleotides bind target RNA, modulating splicing or translation; siRNA induce degradation; mRNA vaccines express antigen; aptamers bind proteins. Treat genetic disorders, infections, cancer.
What is a morphogen gradient in neural tube? | genetics | Neural tube patterning by morphogen gradients: ventral SHH from notochord, dorsal BMPs and Wnts from ectoderm. Concentration thresholds specify motor neurons, interneurons, sensory neurons along dorsal-ventral axis.
How does single-cell epigenomics work? | technology | Single-cell epigenomics assays: scATAC-seq (chromatin accessibility), scChIP-seq (histone modifications), scDNA methylation. Reveals epigenetic heterogeneity in development, disease, and cell states at single-cell level.
What is a genetic network for drug response? | research | Pharmacogenomic networks connect genetic variants to drug metabolism, targets, and pathways. Predict individual drug response, adverse reactions, optimal dosing. Personalize medicine based on genetic makeup.
Explain how sympatric speciation occurs | evolution | Sympatric speciation without geographic isolation: ecological specialization (host shift in apple maggot flies), polyploidy (instant reproductive isolation), sexual selection (mate preference). Controversial but documented cases.
What is a Cre-lox mouse model? | research | Cre-lox system: Cre recombinase excises DNA between loxP sites. Cross mice with loxP-flanked gene to Cre-driver mice for tissue-specific knockout. Inducible systems add temporal control (tamoxifen-activated Cre).
How does DNA damage response prevent mutations? | processes | DNA damage response: sensors detect damage, transducers activate effectors leading to cell cycle arrest, repair, or apoptosis. Prevents mutation fixation. Key proteins: ATM, ATR, p53, checkpoint kinases.
What is a genetic switch for synthetic biology? | engineering | Synthetic genetic switches built from biological parts: promoters, repressors, riboswitches. Design logic circuits for biocomputing: memory elements, oscillators, pattern generators. Program cellular behavior.
Explain how relaxed molecular clocks work | evolution | Relaxed molecular clocks allow mutation rate variation across lineages. Bayesian methods estimate rates from data, accommodating different evolutionary pressures. More realistic than strict clock for divergent lineages.
What is a chimera in stem cell research? | research | Stem cell chimeras test pluripotency by injecting stem cells into host blastocyst, contribute to all tissues. Human-animal chimeras for disease modeling, drug testing, potentially organ generation for transplantation.
How does histone modification regulate genes? | epigenetics | Histone modifications (acetylation, methylation, phosphorylation) alter chromatin structure. Acetylation generally opens chromatin (activation); methylation context-dependent (H3K4me3 active, H3K9me3 repressed). Read by effector proteins.
What is a synteny map for genome assembly? | research | Synteny maps guide genome assembly by aligning sequences to related species with known gene order. Identifies misassemblies, orders scaffolds, annotates genes. Comparative approach improves draft genomes.
Explain how RNA polymerase I differs from II | processes | RNA Pol I transcribes rRNA genes in nucleolus, produces 45S pre-rRNA processed to 28S, 18S, 5.8S rRNAs. Pol II transcribes mRNA, snRNA, miRNA, more complex regulation with CTD phosphorylation patterns.
What is a genetic marker for forensics? | applications | Forensic genetic markers: STRs (short tandem repeats) with high polymorphism, analyzed for DNA fingerprinting. CODIS database uses 20 core STR loci. Mitochondrial DNA and Y-STRs for maternal/paternal lineage.
How does genetic assimilation demonstrate plasticity? | evolution | Genetic assimilation shows phenotypic plasticity can lead to evolutionary change: environment induces phenotype, selection favors genetic variants producing same phenotype without induction, eventually genetically fixed.
What is a ribosome-targeting antibiotic? | applications | Antibiotics target ribosomes: tetracyclines block tRNA binding, macrolides block tunnel exit, aminoglycosides cause misreading. Resistance mechanisms: methylation of rRNA, efflux pumps, enzyme modification of drug.
Explain how DNA methylation patterns are maintained | epigenetics | Maintenance methylation: DNMT1 recognizes hemi-methylated CpG sites after replication, methylates daughter strand. De novo methylation: DNMT3A/3B establish new patterns during development. Patterns maintained through cell divisions.
What is a haplotype for ancestry testing? | genetics | Ancestry testing uses haplogroups from mtDNA and Y-chromosome haplotypes, and autosomal haplotype blocks comparing to reference populations. Estimates geographic origins, migration patterns, admixture proportions.
How does CRISPR activation work? | technology | CRISPR activation (CRISPRa) uses catalytically dead Cas9 fused to transcriptional activators (VP64, p65). Guide RNA targets promoter, recruiting activators to increase gene expression. For gain-of-function studies.
What is a genetic algorithm for feature selection? | applications | Genetic algorithms select optimal feature subsets in machine learning: encode feature presence as binary string, evaluate model performance (fitness), evolve toward minimal features with maximal predictive power.
Explain how Hox genes specify limb development | genetics | Hox genes in limb bud specify proximal-distal identity: Hox9-10 stylopod (upper arm), Hox11 zeugopod (forearm), Hox12-13 autopod (hand). Combined with SHH and FGF signals pattern three-dimensional limb.
What is a protoplast for somatic hybridization? | research | Somatic hybridization fuses protoplasts from different species, combining genomes. Overcomes sexual incompatibility. Applications: transfer disease resistance from wild to cultivated species; create novel hybrids.
How does epigenetic inheritance affect evolution? | epigenetics | Epigenetic inheritance provides additional variation, faster adaptation than DNA mutations, transgenerational plasticity. May facilitate genetic assimilation. Controversial extent and evolutionary significance in animals.
What is a genetic correlation with behavior? | research | Behavioral genetics estimates genetic influence on behavior through twin/family studies, GWAS. Examples: genetic correlations between educational attainment and cognitive abilities; psychiatric disorders with personality traits.
Explain how mRNA vaccines were developed | technology | mRNA vaccine development: optimize mRNA structure (5' cap, UTRs, codon usage, polyA tail), lipid nanoparticles for delivery. COVID-19 vaccines prove concept; applications for cancer, infectious diseases, protein replacement.
What is a morphogen in plant development? | genetics | Plant morphogens: auxin gradients pattern organ initiation (phyllotaxis); cytokinins regulate cell division; transcription factors like WUSCHEL specify stem cell niches. Different mechanisms but similar gradient principles.
How does single-cell multi-omics work? | technology | Single-cell multi-omics simultaneously profiles multiple molecular layers: CITE-seq (transcriptome + surface proteins), scATAC + RNA, scDNAme + RNA. Reveals coordinated regulation across epigenome, transcriptome, proteome.
What is a genetic network for cancer? | research | Cancer genetic networks identify driver mutations, altered pathways, and dependencies. Network-based stratification clusters patients by molecular signatures; identifies master regulators; predicts drug combinations.
Explain how reinforcement completes speciation | evolution | Reinforcement: natural selection strengthens prezygotic isolation (mate preference) when hybrid offspring have reduced fitness. Prevents wasted reproductive effort, completes speciation process in sympatry.
What is a Tet-On inducible system? | research | Tet-On system: rtTA transactivator binds tetO promoter only in presence of doxycycline, expressing target gene. Tight, reversible control of gene expression in cells or animals for functional studies.
How does mismatch repair correct errors? | processes | Mismatch repair recognizes mispaired bases after replication. MutS binds mismatch, MutL recruits, excision removes segment containing error, polymerase resynthesizes. Defects cause Lynch syndrome (hereditary colon cancer).
What is a genetic switch for metabolic engineering? | engineering | Metabolic engineering genetic switches control pathway expression: toggle between growth and production phases; sense metabolite levels to regulate flux; dynamic control optimize yield, titer, productivity.
Explain how Bayesian molecular dating works | evolution | Bayesian molecular dating uses sequence data, fossil calibrations, and evolutionary models in MCMC framework. Estimates divergence times with credibility intervals, accommodates rate variation, multiple genes.
What is a human-mouse chimera for modeling? | research | Human-mouse chimeras with human cells in mouse embryos model human development, disease, drug testing. Humanized immune systems; brain organoids; cancer xenografts. Ethical limits on human cell contribution.
How does chromatin state affect disease? | epigenetics | Chromatin state alterations in disease: cancer (global hypomethylation, local hypermethylation), developmental disorders (imprinting defects), neurodegenerative diseases (histone modification changes). Epigenetic therapies emerging.
What is a synteny for gene annotation? | research | Synteny-based gene annotation transfers functional information from well-studied species to newly sequenced genomes. Identifies orthologs by conserved gene order, improving gene prediction and functional inference.
Explain how RNA polymerase III functions | processes | RNA Pol III transcribes short structural RNAs: tRNA, 5S rRNA, U6 snRNA, other small RNAs. Uses internal promoters (A and B boxes), terminated by oligo-T stretches. Less regulated than Pol II.
What is a genetic marker for conservation? | applications | Conservation genetics markers: microsatellites for population structure, parentage; SNPs for adaptation genes; mtDNA for phylogeography. Monitor genetic diversity, inbreeding, connectivity for endangered species management.
How does genetic accommodation differ from assimilation? | evolution | Genetic accommodation: selection modifies plasticity response (slope, threshold). Genetic assimilation: plasticity disappears, trait becomes fixed. Accommodation more common; assimilation extreme case requiring strong selection.
What is a riboswitch in gene regulation? | expression | Riboswitch is mRNA regulatory element binding small molecule, changing conformation to affect transcription termination or translation initiation. Example: thiamine pyrophosphate riboswitch regulates vitamin biosynthesis genes.
Explain how DNA methylation erasure occurs | epigenetics | DNA methylation erasure: passive dilution through replication without DNMT1; active demethylation by TET enzymes oxidizing 5mC to 5hmC, 5fC, 5caC, then TDG/BER removes. Occurs in germline and early embryo.
What is a haplotype for imputation? | genetics | Haplotype reference panels (1000 Genomes, HRC) impute ungenotyped SNPs using known haplotype patterns. Increases GWAS power, fine-mapping resolution. Phasing haplotypes from genotype data using algorithms.
How does base editing work? | technology | Base editing: catalytically impaired Cas9 fused to deaminase converts C•G to T•A (CBE) or A•T to G•C (ABE) without double-strand breaks. Precise point mutation correction for therapeutic applications.
What is a genetic algorithm for neural networks? | applications | Neuroevolution uses genetic algorithms to evolve neural network architectures and weights. Encode networks as genomes, evaluate performance, mutate and crossover, evolve toward optimal networks for tasks.
Explain how Hox genes evolved | evolution | Hox genes evolved through gene duplication from ancestral cluster. ProtoHox cluster gave rise to Hox and ParaHox. Duplication events created four clusters in vertebrates. Gene loss/gain correlates with body plan complexity.
What is a protoplast for genome editing? | research | Protoplasts efficiently deliver CRISPR-Cas9 ribonucleoproteins for genome editing without DNA integration. Regenerate edited plants. Advantages: no foreign DNA, reduced off-targets, applicable to many species.
How does parental experience affect offspring epigenetically? | epigenetics | Parental experiences (diet, stress, toxins) can alter offspring epigenome. Mechanisms: sperm/egg epigenetic marks, uterine environment, parental care. Examples: paternal diet affects offspring metabolism; maternal stress affects fetal development.
What is a genetic correlation with educational outcomes? | research | Educational attainment polygenic scores correlate with cognitive abilities, socioeconomic status. Gene-environment interplay: genetic effects moderated by environment; genes influence environment selection. Ethical implications.
Explain how lipid nanoparticles deliver RNA | technology | Lipid nanoparticles encapsulate RNA, protect from degradation, enter cells via endocytosis, release RNA into cytoplasm. Optimized ionizable lipids, PEG-lipids, cholesterol, helper lipids. Key to mRNA vaccine success.
What is a morphogen in cancer? | genetics | Morphogen pathways dysregulated in cancer: Hedgehog pathway (basal cell carcinoma), Wnt (colorectal cancer), BMP (bone tumors). Aberrant signaling promotes proliferation, invasion, stemness. Therapeutic targets.
How does spatial transcriptomics work? | technology | Spatial transcriptomics preserves tissue location while profiling gene expression. Methods: barcoded spots on slides (10x Visium), in situ sequencing (MERFISH), microdissection. Maps gene expression in tissue architecture.
What is a genetic network for aging? | research | Aging genetic networks connect conserved pathways: insulin/IGF, mTOR, sirtuins, autophagy, mitochondrial function. Interactions determine longevity; interventions (caloric restriction, rapamycin) modulate network for healthspan.
Explain how polyploidy promotes speciation | evolution | Polyploidy (especially allopolyploidy) creates instant reproductive isolation: odd chromosome number in meiosis with parents, sterile hybrid. Chromosome doubling restores fertility, new species in one generation.
What is a conditional CRISPR system? | research | Conditional CRISPR: Cre-dependent Cas9 expression for cell-type specific editing; drug-inducible Cas9 (doxycycline, tamoxifen) for temporal control; split Cas9 reconstituted by small molecules or light for spatial precision.
How does nucleotide excision repair work? | processes | Nucleotide excision repair removes bulky lesions (UV-induced thymine dimers). Recognizes distortion, excises 24-32 nucleotide oligomer, polymerase fills gap, ligase seals. Defects cause xeroderma pigmentosum (skin cancer).
What is a genetic switch for biocomputing? | engineering | Biocomputing genetic switches implement logic gates in cells: AND, OR, NOT gates from promoters, repressors. Applications: biosensors detecting multiple inputs, programmable therapeutics, synthetic ecosystems.
Explain how total evidence dating works | evolution | Total evidence dating combines morphological (fossil) and molecular data in Bayesian framework. Models character evolution, accounts for missing data. More accurate than molecular or fossil-only methods.
What is a blastocyst complementation chimera? | research | Blastocyst complementation: inject donor stem cells into host blastocyst unable to form specific organ (gene knockout), donor cells compensate, generating organ from donor cells. Potential for human organ farming.
How does histone variant incorporation affect chromatin? | epigenetics | Histone variants (H3.3, H2A.Z, CENP-A) incorporate at specific loci, alter nucleosome stability, recruit specialized factors. H3.3 at active genes; CENP-A at centromeres; H2A.Z at promoters, ambiguous effect.
What is a synteny for evolutionary studies? | research | Evolutionary synteny studies compare gene order across species to infer rearrangement history: inversions, translocations, fissions/fusions. Rates vary: mammals conserved, plants rearranged. Relates to adaptation, speciation.
Explain how RNA polymerase termination occurs | processes | Transcription termination: Rho-dependent (bacteria, Rho helicase); Rho-independent (bacteria, hairpin and U-rich sequence); Pol II (cleavage/polyadenylation signal, Torpedo model); Pol I/III (specific terminators).
What is a genetic marker for animal breeding? | applications | Animal breeding markers: SNPs for genomic selection estimating breeding values; markers for traits (milk yield, disease resistance). Genomic prediction accelerates genetic improvement, reduces generation interval.
How does genetic capture work in adaptive evolution? | evolution | Genetic capture: pre-existing genetic variation allows rapid adaptation when environment changes. Standing variation responds faster than new mutations. Example: pesticide resistance alleles present at low frequency before selection.
What is a ribozyme for RNA cleavage? | applications | Hammerhead ribozyme engineered to cleave specific RNA sequences. Therapeutic potential: anti-HIV ribozymes in clinical trials; self-cleaving ribozymes in synthetic circuits for RNA processing, regulation.
Explain how imprinting is established | epigenetics | Genomic imprinting established during gametogenesis: DNA methylation marks set differentially in sperm vs egg by DNMT3A/DNMT3L. Maintained after fertilization, resistant to global demethylation in early embryo.
What is a haplotype phasing method? | genetics | Haplotype phasing determines which alleles are on same chromosome. Methods: family trios (Mendelian inheritance), population-based (statistical inference), long-read sequencing, strand-specific sequencing. Important for imputation, disease mapping.
How does prime editing work? | technology | Prime editing: Cas9 nickase fused to reverse transcriptase, prime editing guide RNA contains target sequence and edit template. Nicks strand, reverse transcribes edit, cellular repair incorporates change. Versatile for all point mutations, small indels.
What is a genetic algorithm for robotics? | applications | Evolutionary robotics uses genetic algorithms to evolve robot controllers and morphologies. Simulate populations, evaluate task performance, select and vary, transfer to physical robots. Adapt to environments, damage.
Explain how Hox genes regulate other genes | genetics | Hox proteins are transcription factors binding specific DNA sequences, regulating downstream target genes affecting cell adhesion, migration, differentiation. Combinatorial Hox code activates different targets in each segment.
What is a protoplast for transient expression? | research | Protoplast transient expression: introduce DNA/RNA/proteins, express within hours, assay without stable transformation. For rapid testing of gene function, promoter activity, protein localization, protein-protein interactions.
How does transgenerational epigenetic inheritance occur in mammals? | epigenetics | Mammalian transgenerational epigenetic inheritance controversial due to germline reprogramming. Possible mechanisms: DNA methylation escapees (imprinted loci, retrotransposons), histone retention, small RNAs. Evidence from environmental exposures.
What is a genetic correlation with disease risk? | research | Genetic correlation between diseases reveals shared etiology: autoimmune diseases (common immune pathways); psychiatric disorders (neuronal development genes). Suggests common therapeutic targets, pleiotropic drug effects.
Explain how self-amplifying RNA vaccines work | technology | Self-amplifying RNA vaccines encode viral replicase, amplify intracellularly, produce more antigen from less initial RNA. Longer-lasting expression, lower dose needed. Based on alphavirus replicon technology.
What is a morphogen in regeneration? | genetics | Regeneration morphogens: Wnt/β-catenin signals for blastema formation in planaria, zebrafish; BMP patterns dorsal-ventral in limb regeneration; retinoic acid respecifies position. Re-deploy developmental pathways.
How does single-cell lineage tracing work? | technology | Single-cell lineage tracing: barcode cells with heritable marks (DNA sequences, CRISPR scars), track progeny. Methods: lentiviral barcoding, CRISPR-Cas9 recording (LINNAEUS, MEMOIR). Reconstruct lineage trees in development, cancer.
What is a genetic network for drug discovery? | research | Network pharmacology maps drug-target interactions, side effects, disease modules. Polypharmacology: drugs hitting multiple network nodes often more effective. Repurposing drugs based on network proximity to disease genes.
Explain how chromosomal inversions contribute to speciation | evolution | Chromosomal inversions suppress recombination, keeping adaptive allele combinations together. In heterokaryotypes, reduced fertility promotes reproductive isolation. Examples: Drosophila, mosquitoes, sunflowers.
What is a light-inducible CRISPR system? | research | Light-inducible CRISPR: Cas9 fused to light-sensitive domains (LOV, CRY2) that dimerize with partners upon blue light. Spatial-temporal control with light patterns. Optogenetic precision for developmental studies.
How does base excision repair work? | processes | Base excision repair removes small base lesions (oxidized, alkylated). Glycosylase removes damaged base, AP endonuclease nicks, polymerase adds 1-2 nucleotides, ligase seals. Short-patch (1 nt) or long-patch (2-10 nt).
What is a genetic switch for population control? | engineering | Gene drives use CRISPR to bias inheritance, spreading desired genes through populations. Applications: malaria mosquito control (sterility, parasite resistance). Ecological risks, containment strategies under development.
Explain how fossil calibration improves molecular dating | evolution | Fossil calibrations provide minimum age constraints for nodes in phylogenetic trees. Soft bounds allow uncertainty. Multiple calibrations from different fossils improve accuracy. Challenges: fossil identification, phylogenetic placement.
What is a human organoid chimera? | research | Human organoid chimeras: transplant human organoids into animal hosts for maturation, vascularization, functional testing. Brain organoids in mice show integration; ethical concerns about consciousness.
How does non-coding RNA regulate chromatin? | epigenetics | Non-coding RNAs regulate chromatin: Xist coats X chromosome; HOTAIR recruits PRC2; enhancer RNAs loop to promoters; piRNAs silence transposons in germline. Mechanisms: scaffold, guide, decoy, signal.
What is a synteny for crop improvement? | research | Synteny between crop genomes and model species (rice, Arabidopsis) identifies candidate genes for traits. Comparative genomics accelerates marker development, gene cloning, understanding domestication genetics.
Explain how abortive initiation regulates transcription | processes | Abortive initiation: RNA polymerase synthesizes short RNAs (2-9 nt) without promoter escape, repeatedly. May regulate timing, check promoter clearance. Transition to productive elongation involves promoter escape, CTD phosphorylation.
What is a genetic marker for evolutionary studies? | applications | Evolutionary genetic markers: mtDNA for phylogeny, population history; nuclear genes for selection tests; genomic SNPs for demographic inference. Molecular clocks, coalescent analysis, selection scans.
How does genetic redundancy affect evolution? | evolution | Genetic redundancy (paralogs, pathways) buffers mutations, allowing accumulation of neutral variation. Can facilitate evolution by gene sharing: one copy maintains function while other evolves new function.
What is a ribozyme in prebiotic chemistry? | research | Prebiotic ribozymes demonstrate RNA world feasibility: self-replicating ribozymes (RNA polymerase ribozyme), metabolic ribozymes (aminoacylase), ribozymes with multiple functions. Laboratory evolution approaches.
Explain how imprinting centers control domains | epigenetics | Imprinting control regions (ICRs) regulate imprinting clusters. Methylation at ICRs controls allele-specific expression of multiple genes through chromatin loops, insulators, non-coding RNAs. Dysregulation causes imprinting disorders.
What is a haplotype for donor matching? | genetics | HLA haplotypes for transplant matching: high-resolution typing of HLA genes (class I: A, B, C; class II: DR, DQ, DP). Haplotype matching improves graft survival, reduces rejection. Family studies identify haplotypes.
How does RNA-targeting CRISPR work? | technology | RNA-targeting CRISPR: Cas13 cleaves single-stranded RNA, collateral cleavage activity for diagnostics; dCas13 for RNA binding (tracking, editing). Applications: viral RNA knockdown, RNA imaging, base editing (REPAIR).
What is a genetic algorithm for scheduling? | applications | Genetic algorithms optimize schedules: encode as permutation, evaluate constraints (resources, time), crossover and mutate, evolve toward feasible, optimal schedules. Factory scheduling, timetables, logistics.
Explain how Hox genes are regulated | genetics | Hox gene regulation: collinear expression along anterior-posterior axis by 3' to 5' activation timing; Polycomb/Trithorax maintain silenced/active states; retinoic acid gradients; microRNAs; boundary elements prevent spreading.
What is a protoplast for virus studies? | research | Protoplasts study plant virus replication: synchronously infected, assay viral RNA/protein, host responses. Bypass defense barriers, uniform infection. For replication mechanisms, host factors, antiviral screens.
How does maternal effect influence epigenetics? | epigenetics | Maternal effect: oocyte cytoplasm provides proteins, RNAs that influence early embryonic development, epigenetic patterning. Examples: maternal effect mutations; maternal diet affects offspring epigenome via egg metabolites.
What is a genetic correlation with substance use? | research | Substance use disorders share genetic risk with psychiatric disorders, personality traits. Pleiotropic genes affect reward pathway, impulsivity, stress response. Informs comorbidity, prevention, treatment approaches.
Explain how circular RNA vaccines work | technology | Circular RNA vaccines: engineered circRNAs with internal ribosome entry site, encode antigen, resistant to exonucleases. Longer expression than linear mRNA. Production: self-splicing introns, enzymatic circularization.
What is a morphogen in tissue engineering? | genetics | Tissue engineering uses morphogens to pattern constructs: BMPs for bone, FGFs for vasculature, gradients to create zonation (liver lobule mimics). Controlled release from scaffolds, spatial patterning techniques.
How does single-cell proteomics work? | technology | Single-cell proteomics: mass cytometry (CyTOF) with metal-tagged antibodies; multiplexed imaging (CODEX, MIBI); emerging MS methods (SCoPE-MS). Limited multiplexing but direct protein measurement.
What is a genetic network for immunity? | research | Immune genetic networks: innate sensing pathways (TLRs, RLRs), cytokine networks, antigen presentation, B/T cell signaling. Systems immunology models response to infection, vaccination, autoimmunity.
Explain how hybridization leads to speciation | evolution | Hybrid speciation: hybrid between two species gives rise to new species, either through polyploidy (allopolyploidy) or homoploid (diploid) hybrid speciation with reproductive isolation from both parents.
What is a CRISPR screen for functional genomics? | research | CRISPR screens: genome-wide library of guide RNAs, infect cells, select for phenotype, sequence guides to identify essential genes. Loss-of-function (CRISPRko), activation (CRISPRa), inhibition (CRISPRi) screens.
How does transcription-coupled repair work? | processes | Transcription-coupled repair: stalled RNA polymerase at lesion recruits CSA/CSB, recruits NER machinery specifically to transcribed strand. Prefers active genes. Defects cause Cockayne syndrome.
What is a genetic switch for environmental sensing? | engineering | Environmental sensing genetic switches: promoters induced by specific signals (metals, light, temperature), coupled to reporters or effectors. Biosensors for contaminants, pathogens, metabolic states.
Explain how relaxed clock models accommodate rate variation | evolution | Relaxed clock models (uncorrelated lognormal, autocorrelated) allow substitution rates to vary across branches according to distribution. Bayesian inference estimates rates and divergence times simultaneously.
What is a human-mouse brain chimera? | research | Human-mouse brain chimeras: human glial progenitors integrate into mouse brain, improve cognition; human neurons form connections. Models neurodevelopmental disorders, but ethical concerns about humanization.
How does RNA modification affect gene expression? | epigenetics | RNA modifications (epitranscriptomics): m6A methylation affects splicing, stability, translation; pseudouridine affects structure; editing alters coding. Writers, erasers, readers dynamically regulate RNA fate.
What is a synteny for genome evolution? | research | Genome evolution synteny: compare gene order across deep evolutionary distances (animals, plants, fungi). Core eukaryotic genes show some synteny; lineage-specific rearrangements; whole genome duplications visible.
Explain how promoter-proximal pausing regulates genes | processes | Promoter-proximal pausing: Pol II pauses 20-60 nt downstream, regulated by NELF, DSIF. Allows integration of signals, synchronization with elongation factors, rapid induction. Release by P-TEFb phosphorylation.
What is a genetic marker for paleogenomics? | applications | Paleogenomics markers: endogenous DNA authenticated by damage patterns, mtDNA for species ID, nuclear SNPs for ancestry. Ancient DNA reveals population migrations, domestication, extinct species genetics.
How does compensatory evolution work? | evolution | Compensatory evolution: mutation that reduces fitness followed by second mutation that restores fitness, possibly via different mechanism. Common in protein structure, RNA structure, genetic networks.
What is a ribozyme for therapeutic RNA cleavage? | applications | Therapeutic ribozymes: designed to cleave viral RNA (HIV, HCV), oncogenic RNA. Challenges: delivery, stability, specificity. Early clinical trials showed safety but limited efficacy; improved designs ongoing.
Explain how imprinting escapes reprogramming | epigenetics | Imprinted regions resist global demethylation in early embryo and primordial germ cells due to specific protective mechanisms: binding of protective factors, differential hydroxymethylation, histone modifications.
What is a haplotype for ancient DNA? | genetics | Ancient DNA haplotypes reconstructed from shotgun sequencing or capture, phased using population genetics methods. Reveal historical population structure, selection, admixture events in human history.
How does CRISPR imaging work? | technology | CRISPR imaging: dCas9 fused to fluorescent proteins, multiple guide RNAs tile along repetitive loci (telomeres, centromeres) for signal amplification. Live-cell imaging of genomic loci, chromosome dynamics.
What is a genetic algorithm for game AI? | applications | Genetic algorithms evolve game AI: encode strategies as rules or neural networks, evaluate game performance, evolve over generations. Evolve diverse strategies, adapt to opponent, no explicit programming.
Explain how Hox genes affect evolution | evolution | Hox gene changes underlie evolutionary novelties: limb loss in snakes (HoxC6/C8), trunk elongation (Hox10/11), insect wing number (Ultrabithorax). Regulatory changes more important than coding changes.
What is a protoplast for organelle studies? | research | Protoplasts isolate organelles for functional studies: chloroplast import assays, mitochondrial respiration, peroxisome biogenesis. Easier fractionation than intact cells, can be permeabilized.
How does paternal age affect mutations? | genetics | Paternal age effect: older fathers transmit more de novo mutations due to continuous spermatogonial divisions. Associated with autism, schizophrenia, dominant disorders (achondroplasia, Apert syndrome).
What is a genetic correlation with longevity? | research | Longevity genetics: genes in insulin/IGF, mTOR, sirtuin, DNA repair pathways. Genetic correlations with age-related diseases (Alzheimer's, cardiovascular). Lifestyle interactions; polygenic scores predict lifespan.
Explain how virus-like particle vaccines work | technology | VLP vaccines: self-assembling viral structural proteins forming non-infectious particles, presenting antigens in native conformation. Strong immune response, no replication. HPV, hepatitis B, malaria candidates.
What is a morphogen in embryonic patterning? | genetics | Embryonic patterning morphogens: Nodal sets up left-right axis; BMP patterns dorsal-ventral; FGF patterns anterior-posterior; Wnt patterns trunk. Gradients interpreted by threshold responses, feedforward/feedback.
How does spatial multi-omics work? | technology | Spatial multi-omics: combine spatial transcriptomics with proteomics (CODEX, MIBI), metabolomics (MALDI, DESI), epigenomics. Correlate molecular layers in tissue context for holistic understanding.
What is a genetic network for development? | research | Developmental genetic networks: transcription factors, signaling pathways, miRNAs in regulatory circuits. Boolean modeling, dynamical systems. Understand cell fate decisions, pattern formation, robustness.
Explain how reinforcement evolves mate preference | evolution | Reinforcement: hybrids have low fitness → selection favors assortative mating (preference for own species). Can evolve through linkage disequilibrium between preference and trait genes or pleiotropy.
What is a heat-inducible CRISPR system? | research | Heat-inducible CRISPR: Cas9 under heat shock promoter, temporal control with temperature shift. Useful in plants, cold-blooded animals. Less precise than chemical inducers but non-invasive.
How does interstrand crosslink repair work? | processes | Interstrand crosslink repair: Fanconi anemia pathway unhooks crosslink, translesion synthesis, homologous recombination restores duplex. Complex coordination of multiple repair pathways. Defects cause Fanconi anemia.
What is a genetic switch for biocontainment? | engineering | Biocontainment genetic switches: kill switches (toxin-antitoxin, conditional essential genes) activated if organism escapes. Auxotrophy (essential nutrient not in environment). For GMO safety.
Explain how tip dating works in phylogenetics | evolution | Tip dating: include extant and fossil taxa as tips, with fossil ages as data. Models speciation, extinction, sampling. Bayesian framework estimates tree topology and divergence times simultaneously.
What is a human-pig chimera for organs? | research | Human-pig chimeras: human stem cells injected into pig embryos, hope to grow human organs. Low human cell contribution currently; ethical concerns about chimerism in brain, germline.
How does histone chaperone function affect chromatin? | epigenetics | Histone chaperones (CAF-1, HIRA, ASF1) deposit histones during replication, repair, transcription. Regulate nucleosome assembly, histone variants, epigenetic inheritance. Mutations in cancer, developmental disorders.
What is a synteny for paleopolyploidy detection? | research | Paleopolyploidy detection: synteny blocks within genome showing duplicate regions from whole genome duplication. Fractionation (gene loss) patterns distinguish ancient polyploidy events. Common in plants, some vertebrates.
Explain how intrinsic termination works in bacteria | processes | Intrinsic termination: RNA hairpin forms in nascent transcript, followed by U-rich sequence, destabilizes RNA-DNA hybrid, causes RNA polymerase release. Rho-independent termination.
What is a genetic marker for wildlife forensics? | applications | Wildlife forensics markers: species identification (COI barcoding), individual identification (microsatellites), population assignment (SNPs). Combat illegal trade, poaching, monitor endangered species.
How does mutational meltdown occur? | evolution | Mutational meltdown: small population accumulates deleterious mutations, reducing fitness, further shrinking population, accelerating mutation accumulation. Extinction vortex, especially in asexuals (Muller's ratchet).
What is a ribozyme for RNA labeling? | research | Self-cleaving ribozymes in synthetic biology: inserted into transcripts, cleave themselves to produce defined ends, remove tags, control RNA processing. Hammerhead, hepatitis delta ribozymes engineered.
Explain how imprinting affects brain development | epigenetics | Imprinted genes expressed in brain affect behavior, cognition: Peg3 maternal care, Ube3a Angelman syndrome learning deficits, Grb10 social dominance. Parent-of-origin effects on neurodevelopment.
What is a haplotype for disease prediction? | genetics | Polygenic risk scores sum risk alleles across genome, weighted by effect sizes. Predict disease risk, but population-specific, require large reference datasets, ethical issues for screening.
How does CRISPR screening identify drug targets? | research | CRISPR screening with drug treatment identifies genes whose knockout sensitizes or resists drug. Synthetic lethal screens for cancer therapy; mechanism of action studies; combination therapy prediction.
What is a genetic algorithm for music composition? | applications | Genetic algorithms compose music: encode melodies as note sequences, evaluate with fitness function (rules, user preference), mutate and crossover, evolve pleasing compositions. Generate novel music styles.
Explain how Hox code specifies identity | genetics | Hox code: combination of Hox proteins expressed in each segment determines identity. Loss of Hox function causes homeotic transformation (posterior to anterior); ectopic expression causes opposite transformation.
What is a protoplast for membrane studies? | research | Protoplasts for membrane studies: patch clamp electrophysiology on plasma membrane; study ion channels, transporters; membrane permeability assays; easier access than intact cells with walls.
How does genomic imprinting evolve? | evolution | Genomic imprinting evolution theories: parental conflict (paternal genes promote growth, maternal restrict), maternal-offspring coadaptation, dosage compensation. Evidence supports conflict theory for some genes.
What is a genetic correlation with income? | research | Genetic correlations between education, cognitive ability, and socioeconomic outcomes. Gene-environment correlation: genetic factors influence environment exposure. Ethical considerations for policy.
Explain how self-replicating RNA works | technology | Self-replicating RNA: alphavirus-derived replicon encodes RNA-dependent RNA polymerase, amplifies itself and antigen gene. Lower dose needed, longer expression, but more reactogenic than conventional mRNA.
What is a morphogen in stem cell niches? | genetics | Stem cell niche morphogens: Wnt maintains intestinal stem cells; SHH in hair follicle; BMP gradients in Drosophila germarium. Create microenvironment maintaining stemness, regulating differentiation.
How does single-cell epigenetics reveal dynamics? | technology | Single-cell epigenetics reveals dynamics: ATAC-seq on time course shows chromatin accessibility changes during differentiation; DNA methylation heterogeneity in cancer; histone modification dynamics in development.
What is a genetic network for metabolism? | research | Metabolic networks: enzyme-reaction graphs, flux balance analysis. Integrate genomics, metabolomics. Understand metabolic diseases, engineer microbes for production, predict drug effects on metabolism.
Explain how chromosomal fusions drive speciation | evolution | Chromosomal fusions reduce fertility in heterozygotes due to meiotic problems. Can initiate speciation if fixed in subpopulation. Example: house mouse races with different Robertsonian fusions.
What is a drug-inducible degron for protein control? | research | Degron systems: fuse protein of interest to degron, small molecule (auxin, shield-1) induces degradation. Rapid protein depletion for functional studies, complementary to genetic knockout.
How does transcription factor binding specificity arise? | genetics | Transcription factor specificity: DNA-binding domain recognizes sequence motifs; cooperativity with other factors; chromatin accessibility; post-translational modifications; pioneer factors open chromatin.
What is a genetic switch for bioremediation? | engineering | Bioremediation genetic switches: sense pollutant (aromatics, heavy metals), activate degradation pathways. Optimize expression levels, avoid metabolic burden, ensure survival in environment.
Explain how total-group dating includes fossils | evolution | Total-group dating: crown group (last common ancestor of living species) and stem group (extinct lineages branching before crown). Fossils placed on stem lineages, providing minimum ages for nodes.
What is a human-monkey chimera for research? | research | Human-monkey chimeras: human stem cells into monkey embryos, higher contribution due to evolutionary proximity. Study early human development, organ generation, ethical boundaries.
How does RNA modification affect splicing? | epigenetics | RNA modifications regulate splicing: m6A recruits reader proteins affecting splice site selection; pseudouridine affects RNA structure; dynamic during development, tissue-specific alternative splicing.
What is a synteny for adaptation genomics? | research | Adaptation genomics: syntenic regions under selection in multiple populations indicate parallel evolution. Identify conserved adaptive loci. Compare across species adapting to similar environments.
Explain how paused Pol II allows rapid response | processes | Paused Pol II at promoters allows rapid gene activation upon signal: already recruited, just need release. Examples: heat shock genes, immediate early genes in neurons, developmental cues.
What is a genetic marker for domestication? | applications | Domestication markers: selective sweep regions in crop/animal genomes, fixed differences from wild ancestors. Identify genes for key traits (seed size, tameness). Understand domestication history.
How does background selection affect diversity? | evolution | Background selection: purifying selection against deleterious mutations reduces neutral diversity at linked sites. Creates correlation between selection strength and genetic diversity along genome.
What is a ribozyme for RNA circularization? | research | Ribozymes for RNA circularization: group I introns self-splice to produce circular RNA. Engineered for stable RNA constructs, ribozyme itself can be circular. Applications in synthetic biology, therapeutics.
Explain how imprinting affects placental development | epigenetics | Imprinted genes crucial for placental development: Igf2 promotes growth, Phlda2 restricts it. Parental conflict theory: paternal genes maximize resource transfer, maternal genes limit it to conserve resources.
What is a haplotype for ancestry painting? | genetics | Ancestry painting: assign chromosomal segments to ancestral populations based on haplotype reference panels. Visualize admixture patterns, identify recent introgression, study population history.
How does CRISPR base editing treat disease? | technology | CRISPR base editing corrects point mutations in vivo: in mouse models of progeria, sickle cell, tyrosinemia. Delivery challenges: lipid nanoparticles, viral vectors. First clinical trials starting.
What is a genetic algorithm for art generation? | applications | Generative art with genetic algorithms: evolve images, animations, 3D forms. Fitness by aesthetic rules or user selection. Create novel art styles, interactive evolution.
Explain how Hox genes are involved in cancer | genetics | Hox genes dysregulated in cancer: some act as oncogenes (HOXA9 in leukemia), others as tumor suppressors (HOXA5 in breast cancer). Altered expression patterns, involved in metastasis, stemness.
What is a protoplast for protein localization? | research | Protoplast transient expression for protein localization: fluorescent fusions, confocal microscopy. Study subcellular targeting (nucleus, chloroplast, membrane), protein dynamics, organelle markers.
How does genomic imprinting affect behavior? | epigenetics | Imprinted genes influence behavior: Peg3 maternal care in mice; Grb10 aggression; Ube3a Angelman syndrome happy demeanor. Parent-of-origin effects on social behaviors, possibly through hypothalamic expression.
What is a genetic correlation with political views? | research | Political orientation has genetic component (~40% heritability), correlates with personality traits (openness, conscientiousness). Gene-environment interaction: genetic effects stronger in supportive environments.
Explain how nanoparticle vaccines enhance immunity | technology | Nanoparticle vaccines: antigens arrayed on particles mimic pathogen surfaces, enhance B cell activation, cross-linking. For influenza, HIV, SARS-CoV-2. Self-assembling protein nanoparticles, ferritin platforms.
What is a morphogen in left-right patterning? | genetics | Left-right patterning: cilia-driven flow in node moves morphogens (Shh, retinoic acid), creates asymmetric gene expression (Nodal on left, Lefty2, Pitx2). Breakdown leads to situs inversus.
How does single-cell lineage inform cancer? | technology | Single-cell lineage tracing in tumors reveals clonal evolution, metastatic seeds, treatment resistance. Barcode tumors, track clones over time, identify drivers, model tumor dynamics.
What is a genetic network for circadian rhythms? | research | Circadian genetic network: transcription-translation feedback loops (CLOCK/BMAL1 activate Per/Cry, which inhibit CLOCK/BMAL1). Post-translational modifications, light input pathways. Conserved across kingdoms.
Explain how meiotic drive distorts inheritance | evolution | Meiotic drive: selfish genetic elements bias transmission (>50% in gametes). Examples: Segregation Distorter in Drosophila, t-haplotype in mice. Can cause infertility, influence evolution.
What is a light-activated Cre recombinase? | research | Light-activated Cre: Cre fused to light-sensitive domain (LOV), inactive in dark, dimerizes upon blue light. Spatial-temporal control of recombination for precise lineage tracing, knockout.
How does DNA polymerase proofread? | processes | DNA polymerase proofreading: 3'→5' exonuclease domain removes mismatched nucleotides. Kinetic partitioning: right nucleotide favors polymerization, wrong favors excision. Increases fidelity 100-fold.
What is a genetic switch for synchronized oscillations? | engineering | Synthetic oscillators: repressilator (three repressors in cycle), activator-repressor loops. Applications: periodic drug delivery, synchronized population behavior, study of biological rhythms.
Explain how fossil morphology informs molecular dating | evolution | Fossil morphology places fossils in phylogenetic tree, providing calibration points. Combined with molecular data in total evidence dating. Challenges: morphological convergence, incomplete preservation.
What is a human-sheep chimera for organ farming? | research | Human-sheep chimeras: larger organs than pigs, possibly easier gestation. Very low human contribution reported. Ethical, technical hurdles similar to pig chimeras.
